WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE IN OBSERVATION WELLS IN THE UNITED STATES IN 1948
Part 4. SOUTH-CENTRAL STATES
INTRODUCTION
By A. N. Sayre and others
The ground-water inveatlgationa of the United States Geological Survey are primarily concerned with the location and appraisal of the ground-water resources of the Nation. They are carried, on principally in financial cooperation with States and municipalities, or at the request of ofier Federal agencies. Most of the investigations have to do with the availability of usable water supplies, but a few deal with drainage, flood control, construction of waterways and dams, mine drainage, and other problems to which the principles of ground-water geology are pertinent. Water-Supply Paper 992 lists about 1,800 papers and reports describing ground-water investigations made by the Survey in cooperation with States and municipalities through 1945.
Significance of records of water level and artesian pressure An essential part of the ground-water investigations la th« measurement of fluctuations of water level and artesian pressure in wells. The rock formations of the earth are great natural reservoirs in which a part of the water derived from rain and snow is stored to supply wells and springs and to maintain the flow of streams during periods of fair weather. Water levels in wells indicate the stages of these natural reservoirs; they show the extent to which water supplies are depleted by draught or. by heavy pumping, whether for public waterworks, irrigation, or industrial uses, and the extent to which they are replenished in seasons cf abundant rainfall or melting snow. The recorded changes of pressure in flowing wells indicate depletion or replenishment of the artesian supplies.

WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE, 1948, SOUTH-CEFTRAL STATES

Annual publication of records by Geological Surrey The regular publication of records of water level and artesian pressure In the United States was begun by the Geological Survey In 1935 and has continued yearly since. This series of water-supply pspers Is In a sense an Inventory of ground-water supplies. Prior to 194C the reeordo were published In * single volume. Beginning with 1940 the records have been published In eix volumes, covering the northeastern, eoutheastern, north-central, south-central, northwestern, and southwestern sections of the country. Hawaii is Included in the southwestern section. (See fig. 1.) The introduction to the chapter on each State contains an rutline of the general program of work and special features of the program in the current year, such aa preliminary reports issued or put on file during the year or of work done in preparation for such a report. A general discussion of the fluctuations of water level and statements in regard to precipitation, pumpage, and other factors affecting water level aro Included. The following table gives the numbers of water-level reports from 191*5 through 1948.

Scope of present volume

The present volume covers the south-central states and gives records

of water level and artesian pressure in about 1,465 observation, wells of

the Geological Survey and cooperating agencies In Arkansas, Louisiana, Okla-

homa, and Texas. Of these wells, 52 are equipped with automatic water-

stage recorders. For some wells not previously reported complete records

of water level are given. For wells whose previous records have.been

INTRODUCTION
.published this volume gives only the current records. If a complete description of a well has been published in a previous report, only the well number or the well number and a brief description are given In this report. This report includes about 6,250 individual determinations of water level and artesian pressure.
Figure 1. Outline map of the United States showing sections of the country covered by the six water-supply papers on water levels and artesian pressure in observation wells in 1948. The shaded section represents the part of the country covere'd by this volume. Land-surface datum Before 1943, in Geological Survey reports 4 the water levels and arte-
sian pressures for some wells were given in feet above or below the measuring points and for other wells in feet above or below sea level or above or below various assumed datum planes. In 1943, it was decided that uniform practice should be adopted. Accordingly, a precise datum plane was established approximating the land surface at each well. The water levels and artesian heads for all wells listed in this report are given in feet below land-surface datum unless preceded by a plus (+) sign, or otherwise indicated in the descriptive text for each State. When water levels or artes(ian heads are referred to land-surface datum for the first time, a conversion factor is given in the descriptive matter preceding them in order to facilitate comparison of the older and newer records.

4 WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE, 1948, SOUTH-CENTRAL STATES
Network of Federal observation wells In 1942 and 1943 the Geological Survey established a network of 60 Federal observation wells. These wells, were selected becnuse the fluctuations of water level in them are believed to 'be typical and represent the fluctuations that occur in the parts of the country in which the wells are situated. At the end of 1948 the network comprised about 360 wells in 45 States, including 300 wells measured regularly in connection with cooperative ground-water investigations. Changes in ground-water level in 1948 in the south-central part of the
United''States In 1948 the precipitation in the south-central section of the country was below normal with the exception of Arkansas and Louisiana. In Arkansas, the total rainfall for 1948 was 50.09 inches, which is 1.64 inches above the 58-year average. In Louisiana, precipitation averaged 56.80 inches, above normal by 0.28 inch. The State average precipitation for Oklahoma was 31.88 inches, which is 1.01 inches below the average for the past 57 years. The average amount of precipitation in Texas was 24.16 inches, or 6.09 inches below normal. This was the driest year since 1924 in Texaa. The fluctuations of both water level and artesian pressure in wells depend, however, on many factors besides the amount of precipitation. In certain of the observation wells there ere fluctuations caused by differences in the rate of pumping or artesian flow from other wells in the area, but most of the observation wells are not noticeably affected by p-wnping or artesian flow. A summary of the changes in ground-water level is given in the chapter for each State.

ARKANSAS
GRAND PRAIRIE REGION
,By J. H. Criner
PROGRAM OF WORK
Measurements of depth to water levels in wells in the Grand Prairie region were made in April 1948 in cooperation with the State Agricultural Experiment Station, the Federal Land Bank of St. Louis, and tl 3 Corps of Engineers, U. S. Army. The measurements were made by Professor Kyle Engler and Mr. Patterson. Measurements of water levels in the Grand Prairie region have been made for 22 successive years. The first measurements were made in September 1927 in cooperation with the Arkansas Geological Survey.
The Grand Prairie region of Arkansas comprises Arkansas County, large parts of Lonoke and Prairie Counties and small parts of Jeffarson and Monroe Counties. Rice is extensively cultivated in the regior. A large part of the water used for riceland irrigation is taken from wells, most of which tap water-bearing material of Quaternary age. A discussion of the conditions affecting the ground-water resources of the region and an account of the early pumping operations and their effect on the general ground-water
1 level are given in an earlier report. Later reports bring tl «»se records up to date and include the records of other wells as measurements are begun. In June 1945 a comprehensive report summarizing all of the works done in the cooperative investigations was published by the University of
2 Arkansas.
1 Water levels and artesian pressure in observation we],Is in the United States in 1935, with statements concerning previous work and results: Geol. Survey Water-Supply Paper 777, pp. 5-8, 1936.

FLUCTUATIONS OF WATER LEVEL The fluctuations of water level In wells In the Grand Prairie region are causer1 , In part, by differences In natural recharge arw? discharge from the aquifer. The water levels In nearly all wells in the region fluctuate as a result of the changes in atmospheric pressure; this mry be as much as 0.8 foot in 24 hours and 1 foot in a few days. Corrections for changes in atmospheric pressure can be made and are necessary to make accurate comparisons of water levels at different times. Measurements given in this paper have not been corrected for atmospheric changes. In the extreme southeastern portion of the region some fluctuation of water levels is caused by differences in the stage of White River. Changes in water levels are also caused by pumping. The water levels tend to stand highest in the spring Just before the irrigation pumping begins, then decline during the period of pumping, the rate of decline being large at first, then decreasing. After pumping ceases the water levels rise, fairly rapidly at first, then at a decreasing rate, and, by spring, tend to stand near the levels of the previous spring. The trend of water level or artesian head from year to year maybe determined by annual measurements made in the spring as late as possible before pumping begins. In some years measurements of water level were made in the fall, but then the general trend of water levels may be masked by local irregvlarities due to differences in pumping. Water levels were not measured in the fall of 1948. Well 280, in the Grand Prairie region, is equipped with an automatic water-stage recorder, and measurements of the water level were given at the times the recorder charts were changed, which was generally once a week. A continuous record of the fluctuations of water level in this well has been obtained since August 1928. Measurements have been discontinued and will not be reported in subsequent years from the following wellsi Arkansas Countyt 205, 293, 362, 412, 475, 492, 601, B 87, B 110, B 171; Lonoke County; 1, 10; Prairie County: 100,
Ashley County A program of annual spring water-level measurements in riceland irrigation wells in Ashley County was started In 1946, and was continued In 1948 in cooperation with the University of Arkansas, Bureau of Research.

ARKANSAS, ARKANSAS COUNTY

.The riceland irrigated with ground water increased from 175 acres from 1

well in 1942 to over 5,000 acres from more than 20 wells in 19^7.

There are three areas in Ashley County devoted chiefly to growing rice,

the largest of which is in Pine Prairie, about 8 miles northeast of Hamburg.

Another area is 4-fe miles southwest of Hamburg, and the other area extends

northwestward from Hamburg for a distance of about 5 miles.

By A. N. Turcan, Jr., I. W. Thrasher, and A. B. Jones
PROGRAM OP WORK
Ground-water Investigations In Louisiana were continued Ir 1948 In cooperation with the Geological Survey, Louisiana Department of Conservation, and the Louisiana Department of Public Works. The State-wide Investigations, which have been conducted on a cooperative basis slrie May 1938, Include the periodic measurement of water levels and artesian pressures In a number of key wells, and the operation of automatic water-air,c,e recorders on several others. In 1948, 10 wells In Louisiana were selected for measurement under the Federal observation-well program. These wells tap aquifers Important as sources of supply at government-owned hospltrls, army camps, etc.; at Industrial plants considered of primary Importrnce In time of war; or In Interstate areas where the obligation to maintain water-level records cannot be fairly assigned to either State.
The observation-well program In Louisiana for 1948 Included 299 wells In 28 parishes. Automatic water-stage recorders were In operation on 39 of these. Of the recorders operated on key wells, 25 were of the weekly type and 14 of the continuous type. A total of 1,674 Individual measurements of water levele was made In 260 wells during the year. Measurements were made on a weekly basis In 12 wells, at monthly Intervals In 77 wells, at bimonthly Intervals In 4 wells, and at quarterly Intervals In 99 wells. The remainder, 68 wells, were measured at Irregular intervals.
All water-level measurements are given In feet below land-surface datum unless otherwise indicated.
The locations of all observation wells in Louisiana for which records were obtained are shown on the map, figure 2 All measurements were made by members of the technical staff except those for Sc-6, at Nor-co, which were made through the cooperation of officials of the Shell Oil Company.

The Inveatigational program was directed primarily toward solution of the irrigation water-supply problem of southwestern Louisiana. Numerous local exploratory projects for cities and towns required considerable attention. No Important technical reports were published by the Louisiana district in 1948.
FLUCTUATIONS OP WATER LEVEL Northern Louisiana. Levels in observation wells in the vicinities of Bastrop, In Morehouse Parish, Monroe, in Ouachita Parish, Springhill, In Webster Parish, and Hodge, in Jackson Parish, where paper mills obtain water supplies principally from wells, continued to decline ir 1948. At Monroe, where all industrial wells tap the Sparta sands, the lowest level in 3 years of record for well Ou-77 was reached on October 1 -223 feet below land surface, 3.4 feet below the lowest level in 1947. At Bastrop, Well Mo-9, which taps deep Sparta sands (890 feet) showed a decline of 16 feet from the lowest level In 1947. (Fig. 3 .) Levels in shallower (100-foot) wells at Bastrop showed no significant change during the year, although pumpage from the shallow sane? has partially dewatered the aquifer. Conditions in the deeper sands (250 to 400 feet) at Sprirghill remained about the same as In 1947, but pumpage from a Ranney collector caused a decline of about 3 feet in water level In nearby wells tapping the shallow (100 to 150 feet) sands. Supply conditions remain critics1 In this area. Records for well Ja-13, at Hodge (fig. 4 ), showed no marked change In water-level conditions from 1947. Central Louisiana. A total of 132 measurements of water level was made in 16 wells In Grant, LaSalle, Natchitoches, and Rapides Parishes during 1948. Of these wells, 13 were measured at monthly intervels and 1, R-26 at Alexandria, was equipped with an automatic water-stage recorder. Records for .well R-26, which taps the 1,100-foot sand In the Alexandria area, show a decline of about 4 feet from the lowest level In 1947, but conditions are much less critical than in 1943 and 1944 (fig. 5 ). Artesian ^pressures recorded for two wells, which tap an aquifer in the WIlcox formation In the vicinity of the Natchitoches well field about 5 miles south of the city, show a slight rise during 1948. One of the nearby city wells taps this sand.

All supply wells of the town of Colfax now tap a sand and gravel aquifer of Pleistocene age in the uplands about 4 miles east of the town. Water levels in wells which tap this source indicate no serious decline since pumping began in April 1947.
Southwestern Louisiana.--During 1948, 1,231 measurements of water level were made in 181 wells in 12 parishes. Automatic water-stage recorders were operated on 17 additional wells. Measurements were made at erratic intervals in 24 wells, at weekly intervals in 10 wells, at monthly in intervals in 50 wells, and at quarterly intervals in 97 wells. Water levels fell to the lowest points on record for all observation wells in the area. Especially large declines were recorded in the Lake Charles ar"ea; levels in wells which tap the "200-foot" sand fell 8 feet below the 1947 low; in the "500-foot" sand, 13 feet below the 1947 low; and in the "700-foot" sand, 14 feet below the 1947 low.
Wells in the northern part of Cameron Parish showed declines of 8 feet, and those along the Gulf of Mexico, near Cameron, declined 2.2 feet below the 1947 low. In Vermilion Parish, near the Intracoastal Canal, the decline was about 1 foot; at Erath it was 2.8 feet; and at Gueydan it was 6 feet below the 1947 low. In Jefferson Davis Parish, near Fenton, the decline was 6.5 feet; near Iowa Junction it was 13.5 feet; and in the vicinity of Welsh it was 4 feet below the 1947 low. North of Crowley, in Acadia Parish, the decline was less than 2 feet.
These declines resulted from the largest withdrawal on record for irrigation of rice in southwestern Louisiana, an estimated 590,000 acre-feet of water. This was due partly to a record irrigated acreage total, and partly to a severe mid-season drought. Figures 6 and 7 illustrate waterlevel fluctuations in selected key wells in the area.
Southeastern Louisiana. 205 measurements of water level or artesian pressure were made in 48 wells in 5 parishes in this area during 1948. In addition, automatic recorders were operated on 1 well in Orleans Parish, 1 well in St. Charles Parish, and 11 wells in East Baton RTuge Parish.
Water levels in key wells tapping the "cooling water" sands in the Baton Rouge industrial area fell as much as 29 feet below the low of 1947, and were about the same as in 1944, the lowest on record. Well EB-22, about 700 feet deep, taps both the "400-foot" and "600-fooV sands. In July and August water levels in this well reached a low of 232 feet below

the land surface. Figures 8 and 9 illustrate conditions during the past 8

years. The water level in well Sc-6, at Norco in St. Charles Parish, reached
a record low in 1948, about 73 feet below land-surface datum. The accelerated decline of 1947 appears to have run its course, although at the end of 1948 the level was more than 2 feet below that at the beginning of the

year. Records for well Or-25, which taps the "800-foot" sand in the New
Orleans area, show a steady decline in water level since observation began
in April 1945 (fig. 10). The Lowest level on record occurred in late July
1948 and was 85 feet below the land surface 7 feet below the low of 1947.
No significant changes in the artesian pressures of flowing wells in
southeastern Louisiana were apparent in 1948. Seasonal declines of a few
feet during the irrigating season for strawberries and other truck crops
were offset by recovery in the fall.

OKLAHOMA

By S. L. Schoff, E. W. Heed, and L. V. Davls

PROGRAM OF WORK

The observation-well program In Oklahoma, which was begun In 1934,

was continued In 1948 In cooperation with the Oklahoma Geological Survey

and the Oklahoma Agricultural and Mechanical College.

In the Stlllwater Creek Basin, Payne County, where the first systematic

Observations

of water level In Oklahoma were made, the observation-well

program was continued In cooperation with Dr. H. J. Harper, head of the

Department of Agronomy of the Oklahoma Agricultural and Mechanical College.

Dr. M. J. Pllce, also of the Department of Agronomy, who has served as

voluntary well observer since 1940, made 100 measurements of water level

In 1948.

Measurements In an Irrigation well at the Southwestern Cotton Sub-

station near Tlpton, Tlllman County, started In 1944, were continued In

1948. The substation Is operated by the Oklahoma Agricultural and Mechani-

cal College and the United States Department of Agriculture.

The rest of the observation-well program was conducted In cooperation

with the Oklahoma Geological Survey, and Includes observations in the

Panhandle, begun In 1937; Cleveland County, begun in 1939; the North

Canadian River Valley (Canadian, Blalne, Major, Woodward, ant Harper

Counties) begun In 1940; Oklahoma and McClaln Counties, begun In 1943;

Caddo County, begun In 1945; Grady, Bills, and Ouster Counties, begun in

1946; Comanche, Jackson, Klowa, Roger Mills, and Washita Counties, begun

In 1947; and Kay and Seminole Counties, begun In 1948. The locations of

the observation wells are shown In figure 11.

Brief descriptions of the topography, geology and grouni-water condi-

tions of the parts of Oklahoma covered by the observation-well program

have been published In previous water-supply papers of this series as fol-

lows: Stlllwater Creek Basin, 777; the Panhandle, 840 and 8*5; Cleveland 72

COLORADO ___ TEXAS
TEXAS Figure 11.--Map of Oklahoma showine location of observation wells in 1948.

74 WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE, 1948, SODTH-CBNTR'L STATES
County, 886 and 939; North Canadian River Valley, 909; Canadian County, 947; Oklahoma County, 989 and 1026; Tillman County, 1019; Caddo County, 1026; Grady, Ellis, and Custer Counties, 1074; and Comanche, Jackson, Kiowa, Roger Mills, and Uashita Counties, 1099. Kay and Semlnole Counties are described in this report.
During 1948, a total of 1,316 tape measurements of water level was made in 184 wells, and automatic water-stage recorders were operated in 7 wells. Altogether, 207 wells are listed on pages that folio-?, including 21 that were added to the program during the year. Observations were discontinued in 2 wells after several measurements had been made, and in 10 others without measurements having been made. Six wells not measured in 1948 were retained. The number of wells on the active list at the end of 1948 was 194.
The frequency of measurement in the individual wells ranged in 1948 from once a year, as in most of the observation wells of the panhandle counties, to once a week, as in Cleveland County. Measurements were made monthly in the wells in Grady, Oklahoma, and Payne Counties, They were made monthly, also, in Caddo County until November, when several new wells were added and the frequency was changed to weekly. Most of the other wells were measured at irregular intervals.
An 8-page report entitled "Ground-water irrigation in tl 3 Duke area, Jackson and Greer Counties, Oklahoma," by Stuart L. Schoff, rns published late in 1948 as Oklahoma Geological Survey Mineral Report 18.
PRECIPITATION The average precipitation in the State of Oklahoma in 1P48 ftas 31.88 inches', which was 1.01 inches below the 57-year average. Statewide, it was 97 percent of the 57-year average; in the eastern division, it was 100 percent; in the central division, 92 percent; in the western division, 91 percent. Despite this near approach to normal, the precipitrtion "was very unevenly distributed through the State and throughout tl3 year. The panhandle counties and the extreme northwest, and local areas around Brick, Fairview, Geary, Okmulgee, and Vinita recorded annual amounts well above normal. In the southwestern part of the State, annual precipitation was only about 60 percent of normal. Much of south-central and southeastern Oklahoma also was deficient in rainfall for the year....The first 8 months

OKLAHOMA

75

of the year as a whole were wet, especially February, March, June, and July, the last' 4 months of the >year were extremely dry," '

At six stations In the panhandle counties for which records In 1948

are complete, the precipitation ranged from 12.94 Inches (Regnler) to 25. 3T

Inches (Lee Ranch, Guyaon), and averaged 20.78 Inches. Only two of these

stations have records long enough to permit establishment of a "normal"

annual precipitation. The departures from these normals were +7.08 Inches

at Goodwell and -0.23 Inch at Kenton.

At 12 stations along the North Canadian River Valley from El Reno

upstream to Supply the precipitation In 1948 ranged from 21.38 Inches

(Oakwood) to 34.40 Inches (Geary), and averaged 25.90 Inches. Departures

from normal precipitation at eight of these stations ranged from -6.00

Inches at Fort Reno to +10.87 Inches at Supply.

In the vicinity of Oklahoma City and Norman, seven stations reported

precipitation ranging from 27.24 Inches to 34.45 inches. At the three

stations for which normal annual precipitation has been established, the

departures from normal ranged from -5.98 Inches at Norman to +3.08 Inches

at Oklahoma City.

In Payne County, two stations reported total precipitation in 1948 as

31.64 and 32.78 Inches. The departure from normal at Stillwrter was -1*71

Inches .

In the area underlain by the Rush Springs sandstone In Caddo and

Grady Counties, the precipitation at three stations ranged from 19.49

Inches (Carnegie) to 26. S4 inches (Chlckasha), and averaged 23*98 Inches.

Departures from normal ranged from -1.60 to -9.79 inches and averaged

-4.98 inches.

PUMPAOE Records of ground-water pumpage In Oklahoma are best for the panhandle counties, from which reports of municipal pumpage for the larger towns are furnished regularly through the courtesy of the Southwestern Public Service Company. The total pumpage for seven towns In the Panhandle was about 13 percent greater In 1948 than in 1947. The largest increase ras about 50 percent at Felt. Pumpage Increased about 32.5 percent at Terhoma and 18.5 percent at Ouymon. The quantity of water pumped for Irrigation and other uses by the Panhandle Agricultural and Mechanical College decreased about 43 percent, and this, coupled with small declines elsewhere, resulted in a
2 decrease of about 4 percent for the area as a whole. Including the estimated pumpage of industries not supplied with water from municipal systems and pumpage for irrigation, the total for 1948 Is 829,777,000 gallons, or about 2,546 acre-feet.

Panhandle Despite relatively dry weather during several preceding months, the amount of ground water stored in the principal water-bearing formations ol the panhandle counties (Beaver, Texas, and Cimarron) was grerter in May 1948 than at any time during the 10 years of record. Measurements of water level In 51 observation wells were made about the middle of May, and an-average, taking into account the relative importance of several different water-bearing formations, shows tl 't the water stood about 0.5 foot higher than In October 1947, when the Irst previous measurements were made, and more than 3 feet higher than in F-wember 1938, when the lowest level was recorded* 2 Total pumpage in 1947, as given in Water-Supply Paper 1099, is In error. Figures shown are for Texas County alone. Pumpage for entire area in 1947 was 868,073,000 gallons, or 2,664 acre-feet.

OKLAHOMA

77

Over the period of record, the rise in water levels has been greatest
in Cimarron County, where, however, the average for May,1948 was 0.05 foot
lower than in October 1947. This decline is small and does not indicate the
beginning of a persistent decline. On the contrary, it is traceable to the
relatively low stage of water levels in observation wells tapping the allu-
vium- This aauifer occurs in only a small part of the area, rnd accordingly
is given small weight in the average, but the water levels in it declined an
average of nearly 3 feet between October and May, offsetting an average rise
of 0.73 foot in the observation wells on the upland plains. V'ater levels in
wells in alluvium in Oklahoma, 1944-48, are shown in figure 12. The average for Texas County, which has shown the smallest rise during
the period of record, was 0.24 foot above the previous high level and a
total of 2.55 feet above the record low of November 1939. In the observa-
tion wells on the upland plains, the average of the water levels was the
highest yet recorded, but for wells along the major streams the average
was intermediate between the highest and lowest on record.
The average for Beaver County was nearly half a foot above the pre-*
vioua high level, recorded in April 1947. The average for wells In the
northwestern part of the county exceeded the previous record by 0.31 foot,
and the average for the southeastern part was 0.96 foot above the previous
high level, but, as In the other two counties, the water levels in wells
along the valleys were at intermediate stages.

North Canadian River Valley The observation wells representing ground water along tl <? North Canadian River Valley extend from the vicinity of Yukon, near the line between Oklahoma and Canadian Counties, upstream to Beaver. Some of them are close enough to the channel so that the water levels in them respond more or less promptly to stages of the river. In others, farther from the channel, the fluctuations are caused principally by variations In recharge to and discharge from the ground-water reservoir. In 1948 tl^ number of measurements,of water level ranged from 1 to 53 a well. Becruse of the diverse ground-water conditions represented and the wide range in the frequency of measurements in individual wells, the observed water-level fluctuations seem to follow no common pattern. In three wells close to the channel, in Blaine and Major Counties, th» highest recorded water level for the year occurred on May 17. In two of these wells the lowest recorded level occurred on February 2?, but in the other it came on October 7. In the two wells In Woodward County that are near the cl^nnel, the highest recorded level for the year occurred on February 23, but the lowest levels came 5 months apart May 17 and October 7. The other two wells near the channel are in Canadian County. One is affected by nearby heavy pumping, and in it the highest recorded water level occurred in March and the lowest in January. In the other well, the highest water level came In October and the lowest in February* In wells farther from the channel, the highest recorded water levels occurred as follows: In April, four wells; in May, one well; in July, one wellf in October, three wells. For the same group of wells, the lowest

80 WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE, 1948, SOUTH-CENTRAL STATES

recorded water levels occurred as follows: In February, six wells; in January, two wells; and in October, two wells.
Compared with the preceding year, the water levels In most of the

wells were higher in October 1948 by amounts ranging from 0.07 foot to

2.45 feet. In two wells, however, they were 0.31 foot and 1.43 feet lower

than in 1947. The most complete record of ground-water fluctuations In the alluvium
of the North Canadian River is that of Beaver County well 5Z^, which was

measured 53 times. Canadian County wells 1 and 06 were measured 14 times

each, and Canadian 052a was measured 10 times. Hydrographs for Beaver

County well 528 and Canadian County 06 appear in figure 12.

Cleveland 14and 15 see fig. 12). The water levels ranged through 3.61, 4.51 and 5.08 feet, and at the end of the year they were 0.80 foot, C.92 foot, and 1.13 feet lower than in late December 1947, the average annvil decline be-

Washlta River Valley

The five observation wells in the alluvium of the Washlta River Valley were not all measured with the same frequency, and therefore no simple average of water levels is possible. In a well near Cheyenr*, which is the well farthest upstream, the recorded highest level occurred In February and

the lowest in October. The range of fluctuations for the year was 1.44

feet, and the annual net change was a rise of 0.63 foot.

In a well near Clinton (Ouster 1) the highest recorded stage was in January, but higher stages during the spring may have passed unnoticed due to laok of measurements between February and October. The range in waterlevel fluctuation during the year was 1.56 feet, and the net annual change from October 1947 to October 1948- was a decline of 2.05 feet.

OKLAHOMA

81

In a well near Carnegie, the highest recorded water level occurred on May 4, the lowest occurred twice on September 25 and October 27 and the range for the year was 2.95 feet. In a well near Fort Cobb, the highest recorded water level came on March 30 and the lowest on July 26, the range for the year being 2.94 feet (fig. 12, Caddo 2). The net arnual changes In these two wells were declines of 0.25 foot (Port Cobb) and 1.34 feet (Carnegie).
In a well north of Chlckasha, the water level declined 2.84 feet from the Initial measurement of July 1 to December 28, with only on» small short-lived rise that was recorded on November 23.
Caddo County Only one well tapping water In the Rush Springs sandstone of Caddo County was measured throughout the year. The water level In It fluctuated through a range of 2.79 feet, the highest recorded stage occurring on March 30 'and the lowest on June 30* Measurements begun late In March In four other wells tapping the same aquifer showed water-level fluctuations ranging from 2.25 to 3.59 feet. Measurements were begun, also, In nine ells later In the year, and most of them were measured weekly during November and December, but the records are too short to permit drawing conclusions. Although the Rush Springs sandstone Is considered a water-table aquifer, the fluctuations of ground-water level In It seem to be due In large measure to changes In barometric pressure, as Is true of artesian aquifers* During a 5-day period In Way, a mlcrobarograph was operated near Caddo County well 11, In which an automatic water-stage recorder provided a continuous record of the ground-water level. When converted to the amplitude of a barometer using water Instead of mercury and Inverted, the barometric fluctuations closely resemble the fluctuations of the hydrograph, except that the vertical range Is somewhat greater. Likewise, the hydrog'raphs for wells 11 and 14 for the period December 24-30 are strikingly similar even In small details, although the two wells are 8 miles apart. As no heavy pumping was In progress anywhere In the area and no precipitation had occurred for several weeks, changes In barometric pressure seem to offer the only plausible explanation for the close similarity of the hydrographs. The Rush Springs sandstone Is.very fine-

82 WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE, 1948, SOUTH-CENTR'l, STATES
grained, and pressure changes doubtless move much more speedily down wells than through the Interstices of the rock. The result Is a pressure differential between the water in the well and that in the rock, which is equalized by the movement of a small quantity of water froir the well into the rock or vice versa. This movement appears as a rise or fall of the water level in the well. It Is probably significant that in well 14, where the depth to water is 38 feet, the fluctuations of water level were only about half as great as In well 11, where the depth to water is 54 feet. In other words, the greater the thickness of unsaturated ro".k over the water table the more the changes in barometric pressure are retarded and the greater the resulting affect on the observed water level.
Wells in Caddo County that tap wt.ter in alluvium are discussed under the heading "Washita River Valley."
Canadian County Host of the observation wells in Canadian County tap w«ter in the alluvium of the North Canadian Rival1 . T^ey are considered separately from the group discussed elsewhere under the heading "North Canadian River Valley" because they would obscure the fluctuations in the less numerous, more widely spaced wells farther upriver. Between late December 1947 and late April 1948 the water levels in 29 3 wells rose by amounts ranging from 0.10 foot to 3.98 feet, and in 5 wells they declined from 0.04 to 0.58 foot. The average for 34 wells was a rise of 0,79 foot. From April to October 1948, the water levels in 17 wells rose by amounts ranging from 0,02 foot to 1.25 feet, while in 18 wells they declined from 0.04 foot to 3.52 feet, the average change in 35 wells being a decline of 0.49 foot. The final measurements for 1948 in most of the Canadian County wells were made in October. As comparable measurements were not made in the preceding October, the nearest approach to a net annual change is obtained by comparing them with the water levels in December 1947. During the approximately 10 months of this comparison, the net change of water level in 19 wells was a rise ranging from 0.19 foot to 1.92 feet, while in others it was a decline ranging from 0.03 foot to 1.12 feet. The average change in 50 wells in the alluvium of the North Canadian River was a rise of 0.30 foot. ? Canadian County well 2 is not considered because the water level in It was affected by pumping In nearby wells of El Reno.

Jackson County In Jackson County well 1, which is in the alluvium of the North Fork of Red River, the water level In June 1948 was 0.74 foot lower than In June 1947.
Kay County Kay County ia near the middle of the northern row of-Oklahoma counties, bounded on the north by the State of Kansas, on the east by Osage County, on the south by Noble County, and on the west by Grant" County. Thd Arkansas River flows southward through the eastern part of tl <? county. Approximately the southern half of its course in Kay County constitutes the boundary with Osage County. Salt Fork enters the county near the southwestern corner and Joins the Arkansas at the southeastern corner. The Chlkaskia River, a tributary of Salt Fork, drains approximately the western half of the county. The bedrocks of Kay County are shale, limestone, sandstone and gypsum, of Pennsylvanlan and Permian age. These beds are mostly fine-grained and relatively Impermeable, and although they may yield enough wrter for stock and domestic purposes, they are not likely to yield supplies of water large enough for municipal or Industrial use. Gypsum is soluble, rnd where it is present the ground water may contain high concentrations of calcium sulfate. 4Clark, G, C. and Cooper, C, L., Oil and gas in Oklahoma, Kay, Grant, Garfield, and Noble Counties: Oklahoma Geol. Survey Prill. 40-H, pp. 8-9, 1927; also, Bull. 40, vol. II, pp. 68-69, 1930.

Alluvium underlies the bottom lands along the major streams and in

favorable locations will yield water freely to wells. .A well in the munic-

ipal well field at Ponca City, in the alluvium of the Arkanras River, is

reported to yield 1,700 gallons a minute. Use of the water from alluvium

is limited by high mineral content rather than by low yields. Areas of

terrace deposits at Ponca City, at Blackwell, and north of Blackwell, may

be moderately good aquifers but specific information about t*«>m ia lacking.

Measurements of water level were made weekly In Kay Courty well 1,

which taps water in the alluvium of the Arkansas River, begirning early in

February. The highest recorded stage for the year occurred on July 29,

and the lowest on June 17, the difference being 6.79 feet (fig. 12). As

the well is in the municipal well field of Ponca City, subject to effects

of pumping in nearby wells, and itself is pumped at times, tta fluctuations

of water level are, in part, a response to fluctuations in pumping. The

long-term trends, however, indicate variations in recharge ard discharge.

Kiowa .County

Water levels in two observation wells In Kiowa County were measured in

June. Compared with the stages recorded in June and July of the preceding

year, the water levels in 1948 were 0.81 and 0.20 foot higher.

McClain County

In McClain County well 2, which taps water in the Hennersey shale,

five measurements in 1948 show the highest stage occurring or July 1 and

the lowest on February 27. The range in fluctuation was 8.4f feet.

Well 3 taps water in fine-grained sandstone tentatively correlated

with the Duncan sandstone. The. two water-level measurements made in the

well show a decline of 0.26 foot between April 9 and December 28.

mations, and in all of them the water-level fluctuations caured by seasonal

variations in pumping in nearby wells obscure those due to natural causes.

In well 4 the highest stage for the year was recorded or January 17;

in wells 1 and 3 it was on May 24; and in well 2 it was on December 27.

Wells 6-9 tap water in the alluvium of the North Canadian River. Peak levels were recorded In wells 7, 8, and 9 on March 30, but in well 6 the peak came on July 2 (see fig. 12). Wells 10-12 are In a terrace deposit near Bethany that Is tapped for both municipal and Industrial water supplies, but the water levels In the observation wells respond principally to the shifting balance of natural recharge and discharge. In 1948 the water-level fluctuations ranged from 0.89 foot to 3.44 feet and averaged 2.01 feet. In two of tie wells the net annual changes were declines of 0.13 and 0.17 foot, but In the other a negligible net rise of 0.01 foot was recorded. The average annual net change In the three wells was a decline of about 0.10 foot.
Fame County (jStlllwater Creek Basin) The observation wells In Payne County were measured near the end of each month, ercept In February. The average of the water levels In seven wells fluctuated through a range of 1.38 feet. The highest stage for the year was recorded In July and the lowest stage was recorded twice, In January and December. At the end of the year the average was 0.53 foot lower than at the end of the preceding year.
Semlnole County Semlnole County Is somewhat southeast of the middle of Oklahoma. It Is bounded by Okfuskee, Hughes, Pontotoc, and Pottawatonle Counties. Its northern and southern boundaries are the North Canadian River and the Canadian River, respectively, and It Is crossed from east to west by Little River and Wewoka and Salt Creeks.

OKLAHOMA

87

5 As described by Levorsen, the bedrocks underlying the surface In Semlnole County are shale, sandstone, conglomerate, and limestone, of upper Pennsylvanlan age. Beds of sandstone In the Vamoosa formation crop out in the eastern part of.the county and there receive recharge from precipitation. They dip westward and in the vicinity of the city of fgroinole the water In them is under artesian pressure. It Is of good quality and Is tapped by wells for municipal and industrial purposes. Terrace deposits of Quaternary age overlie the Pennsylvrnian rocks in two small areas totaling several square miles each near Sasal"wa and Konawa In the southern part of the county, and alluvium underlies tire bottom lands along the main streams. The ground-water resources In these deposits have not been evaluated, although It is known that much of the ground water in the alluvium has been rendered unfit for most uses by the disposal of oil-field brines In drainage courses. A water-stage recorder was operated in Semlnole County well 1 from February 19 to the end of the year. The well taps water In the sandstones of the Vamoosa formation, and the water level appears to be effected by pumping in the area, perhaps principally from the Seminole municipal wells. The daily range In water level generally has been 1 foot or more. The lowest daily water level, which Is the level tabulated elsewhere In this report, reached the highest stage for the year on June 22 and the lowest on September 28, the range being 5.04 feet. The water level at the end of December was 0.53 foot higher than at the beginning of the record.
Tlllman County The water level In the irrigation well at the Southwestern Cotton Substation, near Tipton, fluctuated through a range of 3.19 feet during 1948, and at the end of the year was 2.54 feet lower than at the end of 1947, The water level was highest on July 5, but a level only 0.01 foot lower had been recorded on March29. These high levels seem clearly to have been a response to relatively heavy precipitation in March (£..40 Inches) and June (6.08 inches). The precipitation in July and August totaled only 3.46 Inches, and by August 16 the water level had declined 1»23 feet due to natural causes. Between August 17 and September 9, about 8*7 acre-feet of water was pumped for irrigation, and by September 13 the w*ter level had 5 Levorsen, A. TEXAS
By C. R. Pollett
INTRODUCTION
A State-wide investigation of the ground-water resources of Texas has been in progress since 1929 in cooperation with the Texas State Board of Water Engineers. The more or less periodical measuring of the water levels and artesian pressures in selected observation wells has been part of the program from the start of the study. As the ground-water investigations were started in new areas, the observation-well program was likewise extended, especially if there were considerable Quantities of ground water pumped in the areascr an indication that the water level was declining. During 1948, a total of 3,198 measurements was made in 893 wells in 44 counties.
The water-level measurements made in connection with the program prior to 1948 have been published in Water-Supply Papers 777, 817, 840, 845, 886, 909, 939, 947, 989, 1019, 1026, 1074, and 1099. Several miireographed progress reports have been issued which contain water-level measurements and discussions of the fluctuations of water levels in the Houston district, the irrigated area of the Southern High plains, and the Winter Oarden District.
PRECIPITATION For the purpose of summarizing climatological data, the United States Weather Bureau has divided Texas into three parts as follows; Fastern division, comprising the area east of the 97th meridian; middle division, comprising the area between the 97th and 101st meridians; and the western division, comprising the area west of the 101st meridian. The average yearly precipitation decreases from east to west; most of the eastern division is humid or semihumid, the middle division is from semihumid to semi-arid, and the western division is arid. For example, at Bon Wier, in Newton County, in the eastern part of the State, the average
111

112 WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE, 1948, SOUTH CPNTRAL STATES

yearly precipitation from 1914 through 1947 was 55.95 Inches bit the annual

precipitation ranged from 38.21 Inches In 1925 to 81.92 Inches In 1940; but at Kl Paso, In the extreme western part of the State, the avenge yearly

precipitation from 1850 through 1947 was 9.16 Inches, the annual precipita-

tion ranged from 2.22 Inches In 1891 to 21.81 Inches In 1856. A similar wlie range In annual precipitation occurs at most stations throughout the

State.

SUMMARY OF CHANGES IN WATER LFVFLS IN 1948 SOUTHERN HIGH PLAINS REGION

In the Southern High Plains of Texas most of the heavily pumped wells are In the following 17 counties; Armstrong, Bailey, Brlscoe, Castro, Cochran, Crosby, Deaf Smith, Ployd, Hale, Hockley, Lamb, Lubbo-;k, Lynn, Farmer, Randall, Swlsher, and Terry.

Irrigation from wells In this region was started near Plalnvlew, Hale County, In 1911. By 1914 about 140 wells had been drilled In the three

principal dlstrlcts--Plalnvlew, Hereford, and Muleshoe. From 1919 to 1926,

the rainfall In most of the region was above the average and comparatively little Irrigation was practiced. Interest In Irrigation was revived during a period of several years of low rainfall that began In 1927, and, according to the available records, 296 wells were pumped to Irrigate ab">ut 35,000 acres In 1934. Since that time the development has grown rapidly.
The following table gives the approximate number of wells In operation

and the acres Irrigated from the start of Irrigation to December 31, 1948.

Since 1937 water-level measurements have been made periodically in

observation wells in the High plains. Some of these wells are used for

irrigation, some for domestic and stock purposes, and some are unused.

Until 1940, measurements were made for the most part at Intervals of 1 to

3 months. It was found, however, that the most dependable Information re-

garding net annual losses from or additions to storage In ground-water res-

ervoirs In the pumping districts and closely adjacent territory can be

obtained by comparing water-level measurements made each year In the late

winter or early spring before the start of Irrigation.

The largest declines of water level are In four districts; Plalnvlew-

Lockney, Lubbock, Muleshoe, and Hereford. The wells are more rlosely spaced

In these areas than elsewhere.

In a few lightly pumped localities the water levels In 1948 were higher

than they were In 1938 or 1947. The following table shows e summary by

counties of the average net decline In water level from the spring of 1938

to the spring of 1948 In 110 observation wells for which coirparable measure-

WINTER GARDEN DISTRICT Dtmmit, Haverlsx, and Zavala Counties --The irrigation of vegetables has been in progress in the district for about 30 years and, ii. more recent years, citrus trees, feed crops, and cotton liave been irrigated- Most of the irrigation wells draw water from the Carrizo sand which crops out in western Diirmit County, eastern Maverick County, and western end northern Zevala County. In some localities the water from irrigation wells is supplemented by surface water from the Nueces River and impounding reservoirs on Pendencia, Turkey, and Comanche Creeks. Surface water is more economical to pump than ground water, therefore the wells along the river and reservoirs

are pumped very little or none during the wet years. Pumpage inventories that were made in the field Indicate that the amount of water withdrawn from wells was as follows: 30,000 acre-feet in 1929-30; 28,000 acre-feet in 1937-38; and 58,500 acre-feet in 1947-48.
Water-level measurements have been made during July, August, and September, at the close of the season of least pumping when the effect of local pumping is at a minimum. The measurements made during May and August 1948 show the effect of local and seasonal pumping; however, the September 1948 measurements were made after a heavy general rain.

The following table shows the yearly measurements in 47 wells. Some wells are in the heavily pumped areas, whereas others are in the more lightly pumped areas, in the outcrop areas, or in areas remote from pumping. The table also shows the rise or decline in the wells between 1947-48, 1941-48, and 1930-48.

PALPURRIAS-KINGSVILLE R.FGION

For a number of years there has been some irrigation of citrus fruits and some winter garden vegetables In Jim Wells and Brooks Counties. Also, since 1940 there has been considerable development of oil in Brooks, Jim Wells, Duval, and Kleberg Counties. The rate of drilling oil wells has increased since 1942. A gasoline plant southwest of Premont, e naval air base

southeast of Kingsvllle, and a celanese plant north of Kingsville were put

in operation In 1943. These plants originally used large quantities of ground water. In 1946 the naval air base was closed, and in 1948 the cel-

anese plant started using surface water for a part of Its sujvoly. Water levels are usually measured in February each year when the least
irrigation water is used and when the water levels are generally highest.

TEXAS

117

Duval County.--Most of the observation wells are on tt* outcrop area of the sands which are heavily pumped In Jim Wells, Brooks, and Kleberg Counties. The deeper wells In the southeastern part of the county show greater declines than the wells In the central and northerr part owing to the fact that they are nearer the centers of heavy punpage In the PremontPalfurrlas area and the water Is under artesian pressure. In 35 observation wells the average net decline In water level was 1.02 feet between 1947 and 1948; the water level In 5 of these wells rose frcm 0.04 foot to 2.42 feet while In 30 wells the water level declined from 0.03 foot to 4.09 feet. In 31 wells the average net decline in water level was 2.44 feet between 1943 and 1948; In 8 of these wells the water levelr rose from 0.03 foot to 5.66 feet, while In 22 wells the water level decllrnd from 0.20 foot to 15.97 feet.
Brooks County. In 19 observation wells drawing from one or more sands and ranging In depth from 25 to 1,000 feet, the average net decline In water level between 1947 and 1948 was 6-06 feet; rises of C.2 and 0.38 foot were observed In 2 wells and declines ranging from 0.55 foot to 16.40 feet were recorded In 17 wells. Between 1943 and 1948 the decline In water levels In 13 wells ranged from 1.26 to 45.01 feet and avereged 20.63 feet. Between 1932 and 1948 the decline In 12 wells ranged from ].16 to 56.64 feet and averaged 27.61 feet. Some of the wells were In an aree remote from heavy pumping.
Jim Wells County.--In seven shallow observation wells In the outcrop area In the western part of the county there was no net change In water level from 1947 to 1948. The water level In the same wells showed an average net decline of 2.05 feet from 1943 to 1948.
In 17 deep observation wells, mostly In the Premont-FrIfurrias area, there was an average decline of 7.18 feet between 1947 and 1948; the decline ranged from 0.68 foot to 15.39 feet. In 12 of these wells there was average decline of 23.92 feet between 1943 and 1948; the decline ranged from 12.43 to 40.98 feet. There was very little decline prior to 1943.
Kleberg County. In 14 observation wells there was an average net decline In water level of 1.74 feet between 1947 and 1948. In 13 wells there was an average decline of 29.93 feet between 1932 and 1948, the decline ranging from 7.48 to 53.01 feet. In two Klngsvllle city wills, In the

118 WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE, 1948, SOUTH-CENTRAL STATES

central part of the city, the water levels rose 6 and 7 feet, probably

caused by a shift of pumping to new wells in more remote parts of the city.

Hays, Travis, and Williamson Counties. The following table summarizes the

changes in water level from November 1947 to August 1948.

The rise of water level In the Fdwards limestone wells in Val Verde and Kinney Counties was caused by a heavy rain west of Brackettville in July 1948.
HOUSTON-GALVFSTON RFGION The Houston district includes Harris County west of the San Jacinto River, and parts of Port Bend, Waller, and Montgomery Counties. Large quantities of ground water are pumped in the district from irdustrlal and municipal wells in the Houston-Pasadena area and from rice-Irrigation wells In the Katy area west of Houston. All of these wells draw tteir water supply from sands that crop out from 20 to 50 miles west, northwest, and north of Houston. About 230 observation wells In the district are measured

TPXAS

119

periodically at Intervals ranging from 1 month to 1 year. To simplify

discussion the district Is divided Into three general areas the Houston-

Pasadena area, the Katy rice-growing area, the outcrop area of the water-

bearing sands which supply the water, and the Galveston area.

ground water In the district In 1930, 1937, and 1940-48 inclusive.

a The rice wells are pupped only during the season that begins about May 1 and lasts approximately 130 days, and the purrpage in tl 3 Houston and Pasadena areas, although continuous, is much heavier in the summer than it is during the remainder of the year. Therefore, for convenience in compilation and In order that comparisons may be made, the total withdrawals In all three areas are given as dally averages for the entire year.

Houston-Pasadena area.--The preceding table shows that the average

dally pumpage of ground water In this area Increased by 20 millions gallons

from 1930 to 1937, remained about the same from 1937 to 1941, and then in-

creased substantially year to year through 1948. The war Impact was respon-

sible for most of the Increase from 1941 to 1945. At the end of the war,

several plants that had been producing war goods were shut down or produc-

tion was curtailed and there was a corresponding decrease In pumpage of

ground water. This decrease was only temporary, however, and In 1946, 1947,

and 1948 Industry became launched upon an even greater progrem of expansion

which resulted in the record-breaking withdrawals of ground-water year by

year. It was necessary to expand municipal well fields in order to meet the

demands of the steadily Increasing population of the area, ard the pumpage

for public supplies rose each year .to all-time high figures. Most of the

ground water used In the area in 1948 was drawn from about 3]0 wells.

120 WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE, 1948, SOUTH-CRNTRAL STATES
In the observation wells that are screened opposite the heavily pumped sands In the Houston-Pasadena area, the average loss In water level from the spring of 1942 to the spring of 1948 was 48.5 feet. The largest average loss, amounting to 58.8 feet In 20 observation wells, occurred In the eastern Houston and ship-channel areas, where there wan the greatest Increase In pumpage. In the northern, central, and western parts of Houston and west of the city, the loss In water level amounted to 41*6 feet between the spring measurements of 1942 and 1948. For the period 1947-48 the average decline In these localities was 8.0 feet as compared to declines of 9.7 feet during 1946-47, 7.5 foet during 1945-46, and 5.5 feet during 1944-45. The Increase In the rate of decline in the northern, central, and western parts of Houston ie mostly the reeult of the year-by-year increase in pumpage by the municipal well fields. To the north of Houston, there was an average drop in water level of 5.9 feet between 1947 and 1948, as compared with 4.5 feet between 1946 and 1947; 5.2 feet between 1945 and 1946; ani 4.2 feet between 1944 and 1945. The all-time record-breaking average pipage of 145 million gallons a day in 1948 hae caused the decline to continue at an accelerated rate, and spring measurements of 1949 are expected to be the lowest on record for the Houston-Pasadena area.
Observation wells screened in the lightly pumped sands to a depth of 400 feet showed a decline in water level during the period 194?-48, but the rate of decline was considerably less than that in wells drawing from the deeper, more heavily pumped sands. The drop between spring me-?eurements for the period 1947-48 was an average of 2.7 feet. The average drop between 1942 and 1948 was 19.8 feet. In 2 wells for which comparative measurements are available the declines from 1931 to 1948 were S5.4 and 30.1 feet.
Katy rice-growing area. Additional land has been put und«»r irrigation in this area each year since 1935 ae a result of high prices for farm products.. The estimated acreage irrigated by years is as follows: 47,600 acres In 1948; 44,900 acres in 1947; 37,530 acres in 1946; 34,320 acres in 1945; 31,740 acres in 1944; 24,200 acres in 1940; 13,740 acres in 19^7; and 8,000 acres in 1935. During the 10-year period from 1931 to 1941 there was a slow but persistent decline in water level in this area, the maximum being 20.3 feet, the minimum, 5.4 feet, and the average, 11.3 feet in the 12 wells for which comparable records are available. Spring measurements in 1941 and

TEXAS

121

1942 for 44 observation wells showed an average rise during the year of 2.1 feet, due to the small demands for Irrigation In 1941 and the unusually large amount of recharge from heavy rains from November 1940 to November 1941. Little net change occurred In 1942-43. Prom the spring of 1943 to the spring of 1948, an average net decline of 5.9 feet was recorded In 33 observation wells. For the 17-year period, 1931-48, the 11 wells for which comparable measurements are available showed an average net decline of 15.1 feet.
The pumpage for rice Irrigation In 1948 amounted to an all-time recordbreaking average of 120 million gallons a day as a result of an abnormally dry season together with the Increased acreage fanned. The water levels conaecuently declined at a faster rate than In previous years, and It Is expected that spring measurements In 1949 will show the lowest stage on record for the area.
Outcrop area of the water-bearing sands. The water talle In the outcrop area of the aaulfers that supply the municipal, Industrial, and Irrigation wells of the Houston district WBS from 5 to 10 feet 1 Igher during the period 1941-46 than during the period 1938-40, and was ts high or higher than It was In 1931-34. An additional rise was recorded during 1946-47 as a result of the above-normal rainfall during the year. The data Indicate that during the period of record more water was contributed to storage In the outcrop area than moved down the dip toward the pumped rreas. The abnormally wet year from November 1940 to November 1941 and the above-normal rainfall of 1945-46 accounted for most of the recharge. During 1947 and 1948 the wa£er levels dropped persistently. In 10 representative observation wells the drop from January 1947 to January 1948 ranged from about 2 feet to 19.2 feet and averaged 6 feet.
Galveston area.--Prior to 1944 there was a persistent drop In water levels In Galveston County as a result of heavy pumping In the Texas City Industrial area and In the Alta Loma well field of the city of Qalveston. The rate of decline decreased from 1944 to 1947, and at Texrs City the artesian pressure became almost stationary as the cone of depression caused by the pumping became flatter and larger each year- With the appreciable curtailment of pumpage In 1948, as a result of a surface-water supply being made available to the Texas City area, there has been some recovery In

122 WATER LEVELS AND ARTESIAN PRESSURE, 1948, SOUTH-CENTRAL STATES
the water level In and near that Industrial area. Water levels still con-
tinued to decline slowly, however, throughout a large part of Galveston
County during 1948.
